.navbar-ct-full{font-size:114%;margin:0 7em}.mw-parser-output .navbar-ct-mini{font-size:114%;margin:0 4em}</style><div class="navbar plainlinks hlist navbar-mini"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Literature" title="Template:Literature"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Literature" title="Template href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Literature&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>A <b>book</b> is a medium for recording <a href="/wiki/Information" title="Information">information</a> in the form of <a href="/wiki/Writing" title="Writing">writing</a> or <a href="/wiki/Image" title="Image">images</a>, typically composed of many <a href="/wiki/Page_(paper)" title="Page (paper)">pages</a> (made of <a href="/wiki/Papyrus" title="Papyrus">papyrus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Parchment" title="Parchment">parchment</a>, <a href="/wiki/Vellum" title="Vellum">vellum</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Paper" title="Paper">paper</a>) <a href="/wiki/Bookbinding" title="Bookbinding">bound</a> together and protected by a <a href="/wiki/Book_cover" title="Book cover">cover</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">[1]</a></sup> The technical term for this physical arrangement is <i><a href="/wiki/Codex" title="Codex">codex</a></i> (plural, <i>codices</i>). In the history of hand-held physical supports for extended written compositions or records, the codex replaces its predecessor, the <a href="/wiki/Scroll" title="Scroll">scroll</a>. A single sheet in a codex is a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Recto" title="Recto">leaf</a> and each side of a leaf is a <a href="/wiki/Page_(paper)" title="Page (paper)">page</a>.
</p><p>As an intellectual object, a book is prototypically a composition of such great length that it takes a considerable investment of time to compose and still considered as an investment of time to read. In a restricted sense, a book is a self-sufficient section or part of a longer composition, a usage reflecting that, in antiquity, long works had to be written on several scrolls and each scroll had to be identified by the book it contained. Each part of <a href="/wiki/Aristotle" title="Aristotle">Aristotle</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/Physics_(Aristotle)" title="Physics (Aristotle)">Physics</a></i> is called a book. In an unrestricted sense, a book is the compositional whole of which such sections, whether called books or <a class="mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Chapter" title="Chapter">chapters</a> or parts, are parts.
</p><p>The intellectual content in a physical book need not be a composition, nor even be called a book. Books can consist only of drawings, engravings or photographs, crossword puzzles or cut-out dolls. In a physical book, the pages can be left blank or can feature an abstract set of lines to support entries, such as in an account book, an appointment book, an autograph book, a notebook, a diary or a sketchbook. Some physical books are made with pages thick and sturdy enough to support other physical objects, like a scrapbook or photograph album. Books may be distributed in electronic form as <a href="/wiki/Ebook" title="Ebook">ebooks</a> and other formats.
</p><p>Although in ordinary academic parlance a <a href="/wiki/Monograph" title="Monograph">monograph</a> is understood to be a specialist academic work, rather than a reference work on a scholarly subject, in <a href="/wiki/Library_and_information_science" title="Library and information science">library and information science</a> <i>monograph</i> denotes more broadly any non-serial publication complete in one <a href="/wiki/Volume_(bibliography)" title="Volume (bibliography)">volume</a> (book) or a finite number of volumes (even a novel like Proust's seven-volume <i><a href="/wiki/In_Search_of_Lost_Time" title="In Search of Lost Time">In Search of Lost Time</a></i>), in contrast to serial publications like a <a href="/wiki/Magazine" title="Magazine">magazine</a>, <a href="/wiki/Academic_journal" title="Academic journal">journal</a> or <a href="/wiki/Newspaper" title="Newspaper">newspaper</a>. An avid reader or collector of books is a <a href="/wiki/Bibliophilia" title="Bibliophilia">bibliophile</a> or colloquially, "bookworm". A place where <a href="/wiki/Bookselling" title="Bookselling">books are traded</a> is a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bookshop" title="Bookshop">bookshop</a> or bookstore. Books are also sold elsewhere and can be borrowed from <a href="/wiki/Lending_library" title="Lending library">libraries</a>. <a href="/wiki/Google" title="Google">Google</a> has estimated that by 2010, approximately 130,000,000 titles had been published.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup> In some wealthier nations, the sale of printed books has decreased because of the increased usage of ebooks.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup> Although in most countries printed books continue to outsell their digital counterparts due to many people still preferring to read in a traditional way.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup>
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Etymology">Etymology</span></h2>
<p>The word book comes from <a href="/wiki/Old_English" title="Old English">Old English</a> <span title="Old English (ca. 450-1100)-language text"><i lang="ang">bōc</i></span>, which in turn comes from the <a href="/wiki/Germanic_languages" title="Germanic languages">Germanic</a> root <span title="Proto-Germanic-language text">*<i lang="gem">bōk-</i></span>, <a href="/wiki/Cognate" title="Cognate">cognate</a> to '<a href="/wiki/Beech" title="Beech">beech</a>'.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Slavic_languages" title="Slavic languages">Slavic languages</a> like <a href="/wiki/Russian_language" title="Russian language">Russian</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bulgarian_language" title="Bulgarian language">Bulgarian</a>, <a href="/wiki/Macedonian_language" title="Macedonian language">Macedonian</a> <span title="Russian-language text"><span lang="ru">буква</span></span> <span title="Russian-language text"><i lang="ru-Latn">bukva</i></span>—'letter' is cognate with 'beech'. In Russian, <a href="/wiki/Serbian_language" title="Serbian language">Serbian</a> and Macedonian, the word <span title="Russian-language text"><span lang="ru">букварь</span></span> (<span title="Russian-language text"><i lang="ru-Latn">bukvar'</i></span>) or <span title="Serbian-language text"><span lang="sr-Cyrl">буквар</span></span> (<span title="Serbian-language text"><i lang="sr-Latn">bukvar</i></span>) refers to a primary school textbook that helps young children master the techniques of reading and writing. It is thus conjectured that the earliest <a href="/wiki/Indo-European_languages" title="Indo-European languages">Indo-European</a> writings may have been carved on beech wood.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup> The Latin word <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">codex</i></span>, meaning a book in the modern sense (bound and with separate leaves), originally meant 'block of wood'.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_books" title="History of books">History of books</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Antiquity">Antiquity</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Instructions_of_Shurrupak,_Sumerian_proverb_collection,_c._2400_BC_-_Oriental_Institute_Museum,_University_of_Chicago_-_DSC07114.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="4388" data-file-width="3326" decoding="async" height="224" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2b/Instructions_of_Shurrupak%2C_Sumerian_proverb_collection%2C_c._2400_BC_-_Oriental_Institute_Museum%2C_University_of_Chicago_-_DSC07114.JPG/170px-Instructions_of_Shurrupak%2C_Sumerian_proverb_collection%2C_c._2400_BC_-_Oriental_Institute_Museum%2C_University_of_Chicago_-_DSC07114.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2b/Instructions_of_Shurrupak%2C_Sumerian_proverb_collection%2C_c._2400_BC_-_Oriental_Institute_Museum%2C_University_of_Chicago_-_DSC07114.JPG/255px-Instructions_of_Shurrupak%2C_Sumerian_proverb_collection%2C_c._2400_BC_-_Oriental_Institute_Museum%2C_University_of_Chicago_-_DSC07114.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2b/Instructions_of_Shurrupak%2C_Sumerian_proverb_collection%2C_c._2400_BC_-_Oriental_Institute_Museum%2C_University_of_Chicago_-_DSC07114.JPG/340px-Instructions_of_Shurrupak%2C_Sumerian_proverb_collection%2C_c._2400_BC_-_Oriental_Institute_Museum%2C_University_of_Chicago_-_DSC07114.JPG 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Instructions_of_Shurrupak,_Sumerian_proverb_collection,_c._2400_BC_-_Oriental_Institute_Museum,_University_of_Chicago_-_DSC07114.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Fragments of the <b>Instructions of Shuruppak</b>: "Shurrupak gave instructions to his son: Do not buy an ass which brays too much. Do not commit rape upon a man's daughter, do not announce it to the courtyard. Do not answer back against your father, do not raise a 'heavy eye.'". From <a href="/wiki/Adab_(city)" title="Adab (city)">Adab</a>, <i>c.</i> 2600–2500 BCE<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>When <a href="/wiki/History_of_writing" title="History of writing">writing systems</a> were created in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ancient_civilization" title="Ancient civilization">ancient civilizations</a>, a variety of objects, such as stone, <a href="/wiki/Clay_tablet" title="Clay tablet">clay</a>, tree bark, metal sheets, and bones, were used for writing; these are studied in <a href="/wiki/Epigraphy" title="Epigraphy">epigraphy</a>.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Tablet">Tablet</span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Clay_tablet" title="Clay tablet">Clay tablet</a> and <a href="/wiki/Wax_tablet" title="Wax tablet">Wax tablet</a></div><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Stylus" title="Stylus">Stylus</a></div>
<p>A tablet is a physically robust writing medium, suitable for casual transport and writing. <a href="/wiki/Clay_tablet" title="Clay tablet">Clay tablets</a> were flattened and mostly dry pieces of clay that could be easily carried, and impressed with a <a href="/wiki/Stylus" title="Stylus">stylus</a>. They were used as a writing medium, especially for writing in <a href="/wiki/Cuneiform" title="Cuneiform">cuneiform</a>, throughout the <a href="/wiki/Bronze_Age" title="Bronze Age">Bronze Age</a> and well into the <a href="/wiki/Iron_Age" title="Iron Age">Iron Age</a>. <a href="/wiki/Wax_tablet" title="Wax tablet">Wax tablets</a> were pieces of wood covered in a coating of wax thick enough to record the impressions of a stylus. They were the normal writing material in schools, in accounting, and for taking notes. They had the advantage of being reusable: the wax could be melted, and reformed into a blank.
</p><p>The custom of binding several wax tablets together (Roman <i>pugillares</i>) is a possible precursor of modern bound (codex) books.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup> The etymology of the word <i>codex</i> (block of wood) also suggests that it may have developed from wooden wax tablets.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Scroll">Scroll</span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Scroll" title="Scroll">Scroll</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:262px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:BD_Hunefer.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="409" data-file-width="1000" decoding="async" height="106" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/BD_Hunefer.jpg/260px-BD_Hunefer.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/BD_Hunefer.jpg/390px-BD_Hunefer.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/BD_Hunefer.jpg/520px-BD_Hunefer.jpg 2x" width="260"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:BD_Hunefer.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i><a href="/wiki/Book_of_the_Dead" title="Book of the Dead">Book of the Dead</a></i> of <a href="/wiki/Hunefer" title="Hunefer">Hunefer</a>; c. 1275 BC; ink and pigments on <a href="/wiki/Papyrus" title="Papyrus">papyrus</a>; 45 × 90.5 cm; <a href="/wiki/British_Museum" title="British Museum">British Museum</a> (London)</div></div></div>
<p>Scrolls can be made from <a href="/wiki/Papyrus" title="Papyrus">papyrus</a>, a thick <a href="/wiki/Paper" title="Paper">paper-like</a> material made by weaving the stems of the papyrus plant, then pounding the woven sheet with a hammer-like tool until it is flattened. Papyrus was used for writing in <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Egypt" title="Ancient Egypt">Ancient Egypt</a>, perhaps as early as the <a href="/wiki/First_Dynasty_of_Egypt" title="First Dynasty of Egypt">First Dynasty</a>, although the first evidence is from the account books of King <a href="/wiki/Neferirkare_Kakai" title="Neferirkare Kakai">Neferirkare Kakai</a> of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fifth_Dynasty" title="Fifth Dynasty">Fifth Dynasty</a> (about 2400 BC).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup> Papyrus sheets were glued together to form a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Scroll_(parchment)" title="Scroll (parchment)">scroll</a>. Tree bark such as <a href="/wiki/Tilia" title="Tilia">lime</a> and other materials were also used.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup>
</p><p>According to <a href="/wiki/Herodotus" title="Herodotus">Herodotus</a> (History 5:58), the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Phoenicians" title="Phoenicians">Phoenicians</a> brought writing and papyrus to Greece around the 10th or 9th century BC. The Greek word for papyrus as writing material (<i>biblion</i>) and book (<i>biblos</i>) come from the Phoenician port town <a href="/wiki/Byblos" title="Byblos">Byblos</a>, through which papyrus was exported to Greece.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup> From Greek we also derive the word tome (<a href="/wiki/Greek_language" title="Greek language">Greek</a>: <span lang="el">τόμος</span>), which originally meant a slice or piece and from there began to denote "a roll of papyrus". <i>Tomus</i> was used by the Latins with exactly the same meaning as <i>volumen</i> (see also below the explanation by Isidore of Seville).
</p><p>Whether made from papyrus, <a href="/wiki/Parchment" title="Parchment">parchment</a>, or paper, scrolls were the dominant form of book in the Hellenistic, Roman, Chinese, Hebrew, and Macedonian cultures. The more modern <a href="/wiki/Codex" title="Codex">codex</a> book format form took over the Roman world by <a href="/wiki/Late_antiquity" title="Late antiquity">late antiquity</a>, but the scroll format persisted much longer in Asia.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Codex">Codex</span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Codex" title="Codex">Codex</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Bamboo_book_-_binding_-_UCR.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2160" data-file-width="1866" decoding="async" height="197" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/Bamboo_book_-_binding_-_UCR.jpg/170px-Bamboo_book_-_binding_-_UCR.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/Bamboo_book_-_binding_-_UCR.jpg/255px-Bamboo_book_-_binding_-_UCR.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/Bamboo_book_-_binding_-_UCR.jpg/340px-Bamboo_book_-_binding_-_UCR.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Bamboo_book_-_binding_-_UCR.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A Chinese <a href="/wiki/Bamboo_and_wooden_slips" title="Bamboo and wooden slips">bamboo book</a> meets the modern definition of Codex</div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Isidore_of_Seville" title="Isidore of Seville">Isidore of Seville</a> (died 636) explained the then-current relation between a codex, book, and scroll in his <i><a href="/wiki/Etymologiae" title="Etymologiae">Etymologiae</a></i> (VI.13): "A codex is composed of many books; a book is of one scroll. It is called codex by way of metaphor from the trunks (<i>codex</i>) of trees or vines, as if it were a wooden stock, because it contains in itself a multitude of books, as it were of branches". Modern usage differs.
</p><p>A codex (in modern usage) is the first information repository that modern people would recognize as a "book": leaves of uniform size <a href="/wiki/Bookbinding" title="Bookbinding">bound</a> in some manner along one edge, and typically held between two <a href="/wiki/Book_cover" title="Book cover">covers</a> made of some more robust material. The first written mention of the codex as a form of book is from <a href="/wiki/Martial" title="Martial">Martial</a>, in his <i>Apophoreta</i> <small>CLXXXIV</small> at the end of the first century, where he praises its compactness. However, the codex never gained much popularity in the pagan Hellenistic world, and only within the Christian community did it gain widespread use.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup> This change happened gradually during the 3rd and 4th centuries, and the reasons for adopting the codex form of the book are several: the format is more economical, as both sides of the writing material can be used; and it is portable, searchable, and easy to conceal. A book is much easier to read, to find a page that you want, and to flip through. A scroll is more awkward to use. The Christian <a href="/wiki/Author" title="Author">authors</a> may also have wanted to distinguish their writings from the pagan and Judaic texts written on scrolls. In addition, some metal books were made, that required smaller pages of metal, instead of an impossibly long, unbending scroll of <a href="/wiki/Metal" title="Metal">metal</a>. A book can also be easily stored in more compact places, or side by side in a tight library or shelf space.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Manuscripts">Manuscripts</span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Manuscript" title="Manuscript">Manuscript</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:RomanVirgilFolio014rVergilPortrait.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1066" data-file-width="1136" decoding="async" height="206" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/15/RomanVirgilFolio014rVergilPortrait.jpg/220px-RomanVirgilFolio014rVergilPortrait.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/15/RomanVirgilFolio014rVergilPortrait.jpg/330px-RomanVirgilFolio014rVergilPortrait.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/15/RomanVirgilFolio014rVergilPortrait.jpg/440px-RomanVirgilFolio014rVergilPortrait.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:RomanVirgilFolio014rVergilPortrait.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Folio 14 recto of the 5th century <a href="/wiki/Vergilius_Romanus" title="Vergilius Romanus">Vergilius Romanus</a> contains an author portrait of <a href="/wiki/Virgil" title="Virgil">Virgil</a>. Note the <a href="/wiki/Bookcase" title="Bookcase">bookcase</a> (<i>capsa</i>), reading stand and the text written without word spacing in <a href="/wiki/Rustic_capitals" title="Rustic capitals">rustic capitals</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>The fall of the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Empire" title="Roman Empire">Roman Empire</a> in the 5th century AD saw the decline of the <a href="/wiki/Culture_of_ancient_Rome" title="Culture of ancient Rome">culture of ancient Rome</a>. Papyrus became difficult to obtain due to lack of contact with Egypt, and parchment, which had been used for <a href="/wiki/Century" title="Century">centuries</a>, became the main writing material. Parchment is a material made from processed <a href="/wiki/Animal" title="Animal">animal</a> skin and used—mainly in the past—for writing on.
Parchment is most commonly made of calfskin, sheepskin, or goatskin. It was historically used for writing documents, notes, or the pages of a book. Parchment is limed, scraped and dried under tension. It is not tanned, and is thus different from leather. This makes it more suitable for writing on, but leaves it very reactive to changes in relative humidity and makes it revert to rawhide if overly wet.
</p><p>Monasteries carried on the <a href="/wiki/Latin_literature" title="Latin literature">Latin</a> writing tradition in the <a href="/wiki/Western_Roman_Empire" title="Western Roman Empire">Western Roman Empire</a>. <a href="/wiki/Cassiodorus" title="Cassiodorus">Cassiodorus</a>, in the monastery of Vivarium (established around 540), stressed the importance of copying texts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Benedict_of_Nursia" title="Benedict of Nursia">St. Benedict of Nursia</a>, in his <i><a href="/wiki/Rule_of_Saint_Benedict" title="Rule of Saint Benedict">Rule of Saint Benedict</a></i> (completed around the middle of the 6th century) later also promoted reading.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup> The <i>Rule of Saint Benedict</i> (Ch. <small>XLVIII</small>), which set aside certain times for reading, greatly influenced the monastic culture of the <a href="/wiki/Middle_Ages" title="Middle Ages">Middle Ages</a> and is one of the reasons why the clergy were the predominant readers of books. The tradition and style of the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Empire" title="Roman Empire">Roman Empire</a> still dominated, but slowly the peculiar medieval book culture emerged.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:CodxAmiatinusFolio5rEzra.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2052" data-file-width="1475" decoding="async" height="237" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/CodxAmiatinusFolio5rEzra.jpg/170px-CodxAmiatinusFolio5rEzra.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/CodxAmiatinusFolio5rEzra.jpg/255px-CodxAmiatinusFolio5rEzra.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/CodxAmiatinusFolio5rEzra.jpg/340px-CodxAmiatinusFolio5rEzra.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:CodxAmiatinusFolio5rEzra.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Codex_Amiatinus" title="Codex Amiatinus">Codex Amiatinus</a> anachronistically depicts the Biblical <a href="/wiki/Ezra" title="Ezra">Ezra</a> with the kind of books used in the 8th Century AD.</div></div></div>
<p>Before the invention and adoption of the <a href="/wiki/Printing_press" title="Printing press">printing press</a>, almost all books were copied by hand, which made books expensive and comparatively rare. Smaller monasteries usually had only a few dozen books, medium-sized perhaps a few hundred. By the 9th century, larger collections held around 500 volumes and even at the end of the Middle Ages, the papal library in <a href="/wiki/Avignon" title="Avignon">Avignon</a> and Paris library of the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Paris" title="University of Paris">Sorbonne</a> held only around 2,000 volumes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <i><a href="/wiki/Scriptorium" title="Scriptorium">scriptorium</a></i> of the monastery was usually located over the <a href="/wiki/Chapter_house" title="Chapter house">chapter house</a>. Artificial light was forbidden for fear it may damage the manuscripts. There were five types of scribes:
</p>
<ul><li><b>Calligraphers</b>, who dealt in fine book production</li>
<li><b>Copyists</b>, who dealt with basic production and correspondence</li>
<li><b>Correctors</b>, who collated and compared a finished book with the manuscript from which it had been produced</li>
<li><b>Illuminators</b>, who painted illustrations</li>
<li><b>Rubricators</b>, who painted in the red letters</li></ul>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Escribano.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="464" data-file-width="600" decoding="async" height="170" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/00/Escribano.jpg/220px-Escribano.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/00/Escribano.jpg/330px-Escribano.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/00/Escribano.jpg/440px-Escribano.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Escribano.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Burgundian author and scribe <a href="/wiki/Jean_Mi%C3%A9lot" title="Jean Miélot">Jean Miélot</a>, from his <i>Miracles de Notre Dame</i>, 15th century.</div></div></div>
<p>The bookmaking process was long and laborious. The parchment had to be prepared, then the unbound pages were planned and ruled with a blunt tool or lead, after which the text was written by the <a href="/wiki/Scribe" title="Scribe">scribe</a>, who usually left blank areas for illustration and <a href="/wiki/Rubrication" title="Rubrication">rubrication</a>. Finally, the book was bound by the <a href="/wiki/Bookbinding" title="Bookbinding">bookbinder</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Milkau_B%C3%BCcherschrank_mit_angekettetem_Buch_aus_der_Bibliothek_von_Cesena_109-2.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="587" data-file-width="453" decoding="async" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/Milkau_B%C3%BCcherschrank_mit_angekettetem_Buch_aus_der_Bibliothek_von_Cesena_109-2.jpg/170px-Milkau_B%C3%BCcherschrank_mit_angekettetem_Buch_aus_der_Bibliothek_von_Cesena_109-2.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/Milkau_B%C3%BCcherschrank_mit_angekettetem_Buch_aus_der_Bibliothek_von_Cesena_109-2.jpg/255px-Milkau_B%C3%BCcherschrank_mit_angekettetem_Buch_aus_der_Bibliothek_von_Cesena_109-2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/Milkau_B%C3%BCcherschrank_mit_angekettetem_Buch_aus_der_Bibliothek_von_Cesena_109-2.jpg/340px-Milkau_B%C3%BCcherschrank_mit_angekettetem_Buch_aus_der_Bibliothek_von_Cesena_109-2.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Milkau_B%C3%BCcherschrank_mit_angekettetem_Buch_aus_der_Bibliothek_von_Cesena_109-2.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Desk with chained books in the <a href="/wiki/Malatestiana_Library" title="Malatestiana Library">Malatestiana Library</a> of <a href="/wiki/Cesena" title="Cesena">Cesena</a>, <a href="/wiki/Italy" title="Italy">Italy</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>Different types of ink were known in antiquity, usually prepared from soot and gum, and later also from <a href="/wiki/Gall" title="Gall">gall</a> nuts and <a href="/wiki/Iron(II)_sulfate" title="Iron(II) sulfate">iron vitriol</a>. This gave writing a brownish black color, but black or brown were not the only colors used. There are texts written in red or even gold, and different colors were used for illumination. For very luxurious manuscripts the whole parchment <a href="/wiki/Purple_parchment" title="Purple parchment">was colored purple</a>, and the text was written on it with gold or silver (for example, <a href="/wiki/Codex_Argenteus" title="Codex Argenteus">Codex Argenteus</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup>
</p><p>Irish monks introduced spacing between words in the 7th century. This facilitated reading, as these monks tended to be less familiar with Latin. However, the use of spaces between words did not become commonplace before the 12th century. It has been argued that the use of spacing between words shows the transition from semi-vocalized reading into silent reading.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup>
</p><p>The first books used <a href="/wiki/Parchment" title="Parchment">parchment</a> or <a href="/wiki/Vellum" title="Vellum">vellum</a> (<a href="/wiki/Calfskin" title="Calfskin">calfskin</a>) for the pages. The book covers were made of wood and covered with leather. Because dried parchment tends to assume the form it had before processing, the books were fitted with clasps or straps. During the later <a href="/wiki/Middle_Ages" title="Middle Ages">Middle Ages</a>, when public libraries appeared, up to the 18th century, books were often chained to a bookshelf or a <a href="/wiki/Desk" title="Desk">desk</a> to prevent theft. These chained books are called <i>libri catenati</i>.
</p><p>At first, books were copied mostly in monasteries, one at a time. With the rise of universities in the 13th century, the <a href="/wiki/Manuscript_culture" title="Manuscript culture">Manuscript culture</a> of the time led to an increase in the demand for books, and a new system for copying books appeared. The books were divided into unbound leaves (<i>pecia</i>), which were lent out to different copyists, so the speed of book production was considerably increased. The system was maintained by secular <a href="/wiki/Stationery" title="Stationery">stationers</a> guilds, which produced both religious and non-religious material.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Judaism" title="Judaism">Judaism</a> has kept the art of the scribe alive up to the present. According to Jewish tradition, the <a href="/wiki/Torah" title="Torah">Torah</a> scroll placed in a <a href="/wiki/Synagogue" title="Synagogue">synagogue</a> must be written by hand on parchment and a printed book would not do, though the congregation may use printed prayer books and printed copies of the Scriptures are used for study outside the <a href="/wiki/Synagogue" title="Synagogue">synagogue</a>. A <a href="/wiki/Sofer" title="Sofer">sofer</a> "scribe" is a highly respected member of any observant Jewish community.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Middle_East">Middle East</span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1097763485" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><table class="box-Cite_check plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><div class="mbox-image-div"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Text_document_with_red_question_mark.svg"><img alt="Text document with red question mark.svg" data-file-height="48" data-file-width="48" decoding="async" height="40" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a4/Text_document_with_red_question_mark.svg/40px-Text_document_with_red_question_mark.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a4/Text_document_with_red_question_mark.svg/60px-Text_document_with_red_question_mark.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a4/Text_document_with_red_question_mark.svg/80px-Text_document_with_red_question_mark.svg.png 2x" width="40"/></a></div></td><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span">This section <b>possibly contains inappropriate or misinterpreted <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wikipedia:CITE" title="Wikipedia:CITE">citations</a> that do not <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wikipedia:V" title="Wikipedia:V">verify</a> the text</b>.<span class="hide-when-compact"> Please help <a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Book&amp;action=edit">improve this article</a> by checking for citation inaccuracies.</span> <span class="date-container"><i>(<span class="date">September 2010</span>)</i></span><span class="hide-when-compact"><i> (<small><a href="/wiki/Help:Maintenance_template_removal" title="Help:Maintenance template removal">Learn how and when to remove this template message</a></small>)</i></span></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>People of various religious (Jews, Christians, Zoroastrians, Muslims) and ethnic backgrounds (Syriac, Coptic, Persian, Arab etc.) in the Middle East also produced and bound books in the <a href="/wiki/Islamic_Golden_Age" title="Islamic Golden Age">Islamic Golden Age</a> (mid 8th century to 1258), developing advanced techniques in <a href="/wiki/Islamic_calligraphy" title="Islamic calligraphy">Islamic calligraphy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Miniature_(illuminated_manuscript)" title="Miniature (illuminated manuscript)">miniatures</a> and bookbinding. A number of cities in the medieval Islamic world had book production centers and book markets. Yaqubi (died 897) says that in his time Baghdad had over a hundred booksellers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup> Book shops were often situated around the town's principal mosque<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup> as in <a href="/wiki/Marrakesh" title="Marrakesh">Marrakesh</a>, <a href="/wiki/Morocco" title="Morocco">Morocco</a>, that has a street named <i>Kutubiyyin</i> or book sellers in English and the famous <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Koutoubia_Mosque" title="Koutoubia Mosque">Koutoubia Mosque</a> is named so because of its location in this street.
</p><p>The medieval <a href="/wiki/Muslim_world" title="Muslim world">Muslim world</a> also used a method of reproducing reliable copies of a book in large quantities known as <a href="/wiki/Proofreading" title="Proofreading">check reading</a>, in contrast to the traditional method of a single scribe producing only a single copy of a single manuscript. In the check reading method, only "authors could authorize copies, and this was done in public sessions in which the copyist read the copy aloud in the presence of the author, who then certified it as accurate."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup> With this check-reading system, "an author might produce a dozen or more copies from a single reading," and with two or more readings, "more than one hundred copies of a single book could easily be produced."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup> By using as writing material the relatively cheap paper instead of parchment or papyrus the Muslims, in the words of Pedersen "accomplished a feat of crucial significance not only to the history of the Islamic book, but also to the whole world of books".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Wood_block_printing">Wood block printing</span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Traditional_Bagh_hand_block_print_master_craftsman-artisan-artist_Mohammed_Bilal_Khatri,_Madhya_Pradesh,_India.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="5760" data-file-width="3840" decoding="async" height="255" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/85/Traditional_Bagh_hand_block_print_master_craftsman-artisan-artist_Mohammed_Bilal_Khatri%2C_Madhya_Pradesh%2C_India.jpg/170px-Traditional_Bagh_hand_block_print_master_craftsman-artisan-artist_Mohammed_Bilal_Khatri%2C_Madhya_Pradesh%2C_India.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/85/Traditional_Bagh_hand_block_print_master_craftsman-artisan-artist_Mohammed_Bilal_Khatri%2C_Madhya_Pradesh%2C_India.jpg/255px-Traditional_Bagh_hand_block_print_master_craftsman-artisan-artist_Mohammed_Bilal_Khatri%2C_Madhya_Pradesh%2C_India.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/85/Traditional_Bagh_hand_block_print_master_craftsman-artisan-artist_Mohammed_Bilal_Khatri%2C_Madhya_Pradesh%2C_India.jpg/340px-Traditional_Bagh_hand_block_print_master_craftsman-artisan-artist_Mohammed_Bilal_Khatri%2C_Madhya_Pradesh%2C_India.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Traditional_Bagh_hand_block_print_master_craftsman-artisan-artist_Mohammed_Bilal_Khatri,_Madhya_Pradesh,_India.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Bagh_print" title="Bagh print">Bagh print</a>, a traditional woodblock printing technique that originated in <a href="/wiki/Bagh,_Dhar" title="Bagh, Dhar">Bagh</a> <a href="/wiki/Madhya_Pradesh" title="Madhya Pradesh">Madhya Pradesh</a>, <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">India</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Woodblock_printing" title="Woodblock printing">woodblock printing</a>, a relief image of an entire page was carved into blocks of wood, inked, and used to print copies of that page. This method originated in China, in the <a href="/wiki/Han_dynasty" title="Han dynasty">Han dynasty</a> (before 220 AD), as a method of <a href="/wiki/Printing" title="Printing">printing</a> on <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Textiles" title="Textiles">textiles</a> and later <a href="/wiki/Paper" title="Paper">paper</a>, and was widely used throughout <a href="/wiki/East_Asia" title="East Asia">East Asia</a>. The oldest dated book printed by this method is <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_Diamond_Sutra" title="The Diamond Sutra">The Diamond Sutra</a></i> (868 AD). The method (called <a href="/wiki/Woodcut" title="Woodcut">woodcut</a> when used in art) arrived in Europe in the early 14th century. Books (known as <a href="/wiki/Woodblock_printing" title="Woodblock printing">block-books</a>), as well as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Playing-cards" title="Playing-cards">playing-cards</a> and <a href="/wiki/Old_master_print" title="Old master print">religious pictures</a>, began to be produced by this method. Creating an entire book was a painstaking process, requiring a hand-carved block for each page; and the wood blocks tended to crack, if stored for long. The monks or people who wrote them were paid highly.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Movable_type_and_incunabula">Movable type and incunabula</span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Bucheinband.15.Jh.r.Inkunabel.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2708" data-file-width="1976" decoding="async" height="233" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Bucheinband.15.Jh.r.Inkunabel.jpg/170px-Bucheinband.15.Jh.r.Inkunabel.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Bucheinband.15.Jh.r.Inkunabel.jpg/255px-Bucheinband.15.Jh.r.Inkunabel.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Bucheinband.15.Jh.r.Inkunabel.jpg/340px-Bucheinband.15.Jh.r.Inkunabel.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Bucheinband.15.Jh.r.Inkunabel.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A 15th-century <a href="/wiki/Incunable" title="Incunable">Incunable</a>. Notice the blind-tooled cover, corner bosses and clasps.</div></div></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Movable_type" title="Movable type">Movable type</a> and <a href="/wiki/Incunable" title="Incunable">Incunable</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Korean_book-Jikji-Selected_Teachings_of_Buddhist_Sages_and_Seon_Masters-1377.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="400" data-file-width="500" decoding="async" height="176" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/Korean_book-Jikji-Selected_Teachings_of_Buddhist_Sages_and_Seon_Masters-1377.jpg/220px-Korean_book-Jikji-Selected_Teachings_of_Buddhist_Sages_and_Seon_Masters-1377.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/Korean_book-Jikji-Selected_Teachings_of_Buddhist_Sages_and_Seon_Masters-1377.jpg/330px-Korean_book-Jikji-Selected_Teachings_of_Buddhist_Sages_and_Seon_Masters-1377.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/Korean_book-Jikji-Selected_Teachings_of_Buddhist_Sages_and_Seon_Masters-1377.jpg/440px-Korean_book-Jikji-Selected_Teachings_of_Buddhist_Sages_and_Seon_Masters-1377.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Korean_book-Jikji-Selected_Teachings_of_Buddhist_Sages_and_Seon_Masters-1377.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>Selected Teachings of Buddhist Sages and Son Masters</i>, the earliest known book printed with movable metal type, printed in Korea, in 1377, <a href="/wiki/Biblioth%C3%A8que_nationale_de_France" title="Bibliothèque nationale de France">Bibliothèque nationale de France</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>The Chinese inventor <a href="/wiki/Bi_Sheng" title="Bi Sheng">Bi Sheng</a> made <a href="/wiki/Movable_type" title="Movable type">movable type</a> of earthenware c. 1045, but there are no known surviving examples of his printing. Around 1450, in what is commonly regarded as an independent invention, <a href="/wiki/Johannes_Gutenberg" title="Johannes Gutenberg">Johannes Gutenberg</a> invented movable type in Europe, along with innovations in casting the type based on a matrix and <a href="/wiki/Hand_mould" title="Hand mould">hand mould</a>. This invention gradually made books less expensive to produce and more widely available.
</p><p>Early printed books, single sheets and images which were created before 1501 in Europe are known as <a href="/wiki/Incunable" title="Incunable">incunables</a> or <i>incunabula</i>. "A man born in 1453, the year of the fall of Constantinople, could look back from his fiftieth year on a lifetime in which about eight million books had been printed, more perhaps than all the scribes of Europe had produced since Constantine founded his city in AD 330."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="19th_century_to_21st_centuries">19th century to 21st centuries</span></h3>
<p>Steam-powered printing presses became popular in the early 19th century. These machines could print 1,100 sheets per hour,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup> but workers could only set 2,000 letters per hour.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2011)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Monotype" title="Monotype">Monotype</a> and <a href="/wiki/Linotype_machine" title="Linotype machine">linotype</a> typesetting machines were introduced in the late 19th century. They could set more than 6,000 letters per hour and an entire line of type at once. There have been numerous improvements in the printing press. As well, the conditions for <a href="/wiki/Freedom_of_the_press" title="Freedom of the press">freedom of the press</a> have been improved through the gradual relaxation of restrictive censorship laws. See also <a href="/wiki/Intellectual_property" title="Intellectual property">intellectual property</a>, <a href="/wiki/Public_domain" title="Public domain">public domain</a>, <a href="/wiki/Copyright" title="Copyright">copyright</a>. In mid-20th century, European book production had risen to over 200,000 titles per year.
</p><p>Throughout the 20th century, libraries have faced an ever-increasing rate of publishing, sometimes called an <a href="/wiki/Information_explosion" title="Information explosion">information explosion</a>. The advent of <a href="/wiki/Electronic_publishing" title="Electronic publishing">electronic publishing</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Internet" title="Internet">internet</a> means that much new information is not printed in paper books, but is made available online through a <a href="/wiki/Digital_library" title="Digital library">digital library</a>, on <a href="/wiki/CD-ROM" title="CD-ROM">CD-ROM</a>, in the form of ebooks or other online media. An <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/On-line_book" title="On-line book">on-line book</a> is an ebook that is available online through the internet. Though many books are produced digitally, most digital versions are not available to the public, and there is no decline in the rate of paper publishing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup> There is an effort, however, to convert books that are in the <a href="/wiki/Public_domain" title="Public domain">public domain</a> into a digital medium for unlimited redistribution and infinite availability. This effort is spearheaded by <a href="/wiki/Project_Gutenberg" title="Project Gutenberg">Project Gutenberg</a> combined with <a href="/wiki/Distributed_Proofreaders" title="Distributed Proofreaders">Distributed Proofreaders</a>. There have also been new developments in the process of publishing books. Technologies such as POD or "<a href="/wiki/Print_on_demand" title="Print on demand">print on demand</a>", which make it possible to print as few as one book at a time, have made self-publishing (and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vanity_publishing" title="Vanity publishing">vanity publishing</a>) much easier and more affordable. On-demand publishing has allowed publishers, by avoiding the high costs of warehousing, to keep low-selling books in print rather than declaring them out of print.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Indian_manuscripts">Indian manuscripts</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Palm-leaf_manuscript" title="Palm-leaf manuscript">Palm-leaf manuscript</a></div>
<p>Goddess <a href="/wiki/Saraswati" title="Saraswati">Saraswati</a> image dated 132 AD excavated from <a href="/wiki/Kankali_Tila" title="Kankali Tila">Kankali tila</a> depicts her holding a manuscript in her left hand represented as a bound and tied <a href="/wiki/Palm-leaf_manuscript" title="Palm-leaf manuscript">palm leaf</a> or birch bark manuscript. In India a bounded manuscript made of birch bark or palm leaf existed side by side since antiquity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup> The text in palm leaf manuscripts was inscribed with a knife pen on rectangular cut and cured palm leaf sheets; colourings were then applied to the surface and wiped off, leaving the ink in the incised grooves. Each sheet typically had a hole through which a string could pass, and with these the sheets were tied together with a string to bind like a book.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Mesoamerican_codices">Mesoamerican codices</span></h3>
<p>The codices of <a href="/wiki/Pre-Columbian_era" title="Pre-Columbian era">pre-Columbian</a> <a href="/wiki/Mesoamerica" title="Mesoamerica">Mesoamerica</a> (Mexico and Central America) had the same form as the European codex, but were instead made with long folded strips of either fig bark (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Amatl" title="Amatl">amatl</a>) or plant fibers, often with a layer of <a href="/wiki/Whitewash" title="Whitewash">whitewash</a> applied before writing. <a href="/wiki/New_World" title="New World">New World</a> codices were written as late as the 16th century (see <a href="/wiki/Maya_codices" title="Maya codices">Maya codices</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aztec_codices" title="Aztec codices">Aztec codices</a>). Those written before the Spanish conquests seem all to have been single long sheets folded <a href="/wiki/Concertina" title="Concertina">concertina</a>-style, sometimes written on both sides of the local <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Amatl" title="Amatl">amatl</a></i> paper.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Modern_manufacturing">Modern manufacturing</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Bookbinding" title="Bookbinding">Bookbinding</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Publishing" title="Publishing">Publishing</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Urval_av_de_bocker_som_har_vunnit_Nordiska_radets_litteraturpris_under_de_50_ar_som_priset_funnits_(2).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2832" data-file-width="4256" decoding="async" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Urval_av_de_bocker_som_har_vunnit_Nordiska_radets_litteraturpris_under_de_50_ar_som_priset_funnits_%282%29.jpg/220px-Urval_av_de_bocker_som_har_vunnit_Nordiska_radets_litteraturpris_under_de_50_ar_som_priset_funnits_%282%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Urval_av_de_bocker_som_har_vunnit_Nordiska_radets_litteraturpris_under_de_50_ar_som_priset_funnits_%282%29.jpg/330px-Urval_av_de_bocker_som_har_vunnit_Nordiska_radets_litteraturpris_under_de_50_ar_som_priset_funnits_%282%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Urval_av_de_bocker_som_har_vunnit_Nordiska_radets_litteraturpris_under_de_50_ar_som_priset_funnits_%282%29.jpg/440px-Urval_av_de_bocker_som_har_vunnit_Nordiska_radets_litteraturpris_under_de_50_ar_som_priset_funnits_%282%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Urval_av_de_bocker_som_har_vunnit_Nordiska_radets_litteraturpris_under_de_50_ar_som_priset_funnits_(2).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Bookbinding" title="Bookbinding">spine</a> of the book is an important aspect in <a href="/wiki/Book_design" title="Book design">book design</a>, especially in the cover design. When the books are stacked up or stored in a shelf, the details on the spine is the only visible surface that contains the information about the book. In stores, it is the details on the spine that attract a buyer's attention first.</div></div></div>
<p>The methods used for the printing and binding of books continued fundamentally unchanged from the 15th century into the early 20th century. While there was more <a href="/wiki/Mechanization" title="Mechanization">mechanization</a>, a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Book_printer" title="Book printer">book printer</a> in 1900 had much in common with <a href="/wiki/Johannes_Gutenberg" title="Johannes Gutenberg">Gutenberg</a>. <a href="/wiki/Printing_press" title="Printing press">Gutenberg's invention</a> was the use of movable metal types, assembled into words, lines, and pages and then printed by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Letterpress" title="Letterpress">letterpress</a> to create multiple copies. Modern paper books are printed on <a href="/wiki/Printing_and_writing_paper" title="Printing and writing paper">papers</a> designed specifically for printed books. Traditionally, book papers are off-white or low-white papers (easier to read), are opaque to minimise the show-through of text from one side of the page to the other and are (usually) made to tighter <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Caliper" title="Caliper">caliper</a> or thickness specifications, particularly for case-bound books. Different paper qualities are used depending on the type of book: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Machine_finished_coated_paper" title="Machine finished coated paper">Machine finished coated papers</a>, <a href="/wiki/Woodfree_uncoated_paper" title="Woodfree uncoated paper">woodfree uncoated papers</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Coated_fine_paper" title="Coated fine paper">coated fine papers</a> and <a href="/wiki/Special_fine_paper" title="Special fine paper">special fine papers</a> are common paper grades.
</p><p>Today, the majority of books are printed by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Offset_lithography" title="Offset lithography">offset lithography</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup> When a book is printed, the pages are laid out on the plate so that after the printed sheet is folded the pages will be in the correct sequence. Books tend to be manufactured nowadays in a few standard sizes. The <a href="/wiki/Book_size" title="Book size">sizes of books</a> are usually specified as "trim size": the size of the page after the sheet has been folded and trimmed. The standard sizes result from sheet sizes (therefore machine sizes) which became popular 200 or 300 years ago, and have come to dominate the industry. British conventions in this regard prevail throughout the English-speaking world, except for the USA. The European book manufacturing industry works to a completely different set of standards.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Processes">Processes</span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Layout">Layout</span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Parts-of-a-Book.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="319" data-file-width="576" decoding="async" height="122" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/af/Parts-of-a-Book.jpg/220px-Parts-of-a-Book.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/af/Parts-of-a-Book.jpg/330px-Parts-of-a-Book.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/af/Parts-of-a-Book.jpg/440px-Parts-of-a-Book.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Parts-of-a-Book.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Parts of a modern case bound book</div></div></div>
<p>Modern bound books are organized according to a particular format called the book's <i>layout</i>. Although there is great variation in layout, modern books tend to adhere to a set of rules with regard to what the parts of the layout are and what their content usually includes. A basic layout will include a <i>front cover</i>, a <i>back cover</i> and the book's content which is called its <i>body copy</i> or <i>content pages</i>. The front cover often bears the book's title (and subtitle, if any) and the name of its author or editor(s). The <i>inside front cover</i> page is usually left blank in both hardcover and paperback books. The next section, if present, is the book's <i>front matter</i>, which includes all textual material after the front cover but not part of the book's content such as a foreword, a dedication, a table of contents and publisher data such as the book's edition or printing number and place of publication. Between the body copy and the back cover goes the <i>end matter</i> which would include any indices, sets of tables, diagrams, glossaries or lists of cited works (though an edited book with several authors usually places cited works at the end of each authored chapter). The <i>inside back cover</i> page, like that inside the front cover, is usually blank. The <i>back cover</i> is the usual place for the book's <a href="/wiki/ISBN" title="ISBN">ISBN</a> and maybe a photograph of the author(s)/ editor(s), perhaps with a short introduction to them. Also here often appear plot summaries, barcodes and excerpted reviews of the book.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ShellyStarks2011_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-ShellyStarks2011-34">[34]</a></sup>
</p><p>The body of the books is usually divided into parts, chapters, sections and sometimes subsections that are composed of at least a paragraph or more.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Printing">Printing</span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Urval_av_de_bocker_som_har_vunnit_Nordiska_radets_litteraturpris_under_de_50_ar_som_priset_funnits.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2832" data-file-width="4256" decoding="async" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8e/Urval_av_de_bocker_som_har_vunnit_Nordiska_radets_litteraturpris_under_de_50_ar_som_priset_funnits.jpg/220px-Urval_av_de_bocker_som_har_vunnit_Nordiska_radets_litteraturpris_under_de_50_ar_som_priset_funnits.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8e/Urval_av_de_bocker_som_har_vunnit_Nordiska_radets_litteraturpris_under_de_50_ar_som_priset_funnits.jpg/330px-Urval_av_de_bocker_som_har_vunnit_Nordiska_radets_litteraturpris_under_de_50_ar_som_priset_funnits.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8e/Urval_av_de_bocker_som_har_vunnit_Nordiska_radets_litteraturpris_under_de_50_ar_som_priset_funnits.jpg/440px-Urval_av_de_bocker_som_har_vunnit_Nordiska_radets_litteraturpris_under_de_50_ar_som_priset_funnits.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Urval_av_de_bocker_som_har_vunnit_Nordiska_radets_litteraturpris_under_de_50_ar_som_priset_funnits.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Book covers</div></div></div>
<p>Some books, particularly those with shorter runs (i.e. with fewer copies) will be printed on sheet-fed offset presses, but most books are now printed on <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Web_press" title="Web press">web presses</a>, which are fed by a continuous roll of paper, and can consequently print more copies in a shorter time. As the production line circulates, a complete "book" is collected together in one stack of pages, and another machine carries out the folding, pleating, and stitching of the pages into bundles of <a href="/wiki/Section_(bookbinding)" title="Section (bookbinding)">signatures</a> (sections of pages) ready to go into the gathering line. Note that the pages of a book are printed two at a time, not as one complete book. Excess numbers are printed to make up for any spoilage due to make-readies or test pages to assure final print quality.
</p><p>A <i>make-ready</i> is the preparatory work carried out by the pressmen to get the printing press up to the required quality of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Impression_(publishing)" title="Impression (publishing)">impression</a>. Included in make-ready is the time taken to mount the plate onto the machine, clean up any mess from the previous job, and get the press up to speed. As soon as the pressman decides that the printing is correct, all the make-ready sheets will be discarded, and the press will start making books. Similar make readies take place in the folding and binding areas, each involving spoilage of paper.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Binding">Binding</span></h4>
<p>After the signatures are folded and gathered, they move into the <a href="/wiki/Bindery" title="Bindery">bindery</a>. In the middle of last century there were still many trade binders – stand-alone binding companies which did no printing, specializing in binding alone. At that time, because of the dominance of letterpress printing, typesetting and printing took place in one location, and binding in a different factory. When type was all metal, a typical book's worth of type would be bulky, fragile and heavy. The less it was moved in this condition the better: so printing would be carried out in the same location as the typesetting. Printed sheets on the other hand could easily be moved. Now, because of increasing <a href="/wiki/Automation" title="Automation">computerization</a> of preparing a book for the printer, the typesetting part of the job has flowed upstream, where it is done either by separately contracting companies working for the publisher, by the publishers themselves, or even by the authors. Mergers in the book manufacturing industry mean that it is now unusual to find a bindery which is not also involved in book printing (and vice versa).
</p><p>If the book is a <a href="/wiki/Hardcover" title="Hardcover">hardback</a> its path through the bindery will involve more points of activity than if it is a <a href="/wiki/Paperback" title="Paperback">paperback</a>. Unsewn binding, is now increasingly common. The signatures of a book can also be held together by "Smyth sewing" using needles, "McCain sewing", using drilled holes often used in schoolbook binding, or "notch binding", where gashes about an inch long are made at intervals through the fold in the spine of each signature. The rest of the binding process is similar in all instances. Sewn and notch bound books can be bound as either hardbacks or paperbacks.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Finishing">Finishing</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Urval_av_de_bocker_som_har_vunnit_Nordiska_radets_litteraturpris_under_de_50_ar_som_priset_funnits_(3).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2832" data-file-width="4256" decoding="async" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0a/Urval_av_de_bocker_som_har_vunnit_Nordiska_radets_litteraturpris_under_de_50_ar_som_priset_funnits_%283%29.jpg/220px-Urval_av_de_bocker_som_har_vunnit_Nordiska_radets_litteraturpris_under_de_50_ar_som_priset_funnits_%283%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0a/Urval_av_de_bocker_som_har_vunnit_Nordiska_radets_litteraturpris_under_de_50_ar_som_priset_funnits_%283%29.jpg/330px-Urval_av_de_bocker_som_har_vunnit_Nordiska_radets_litteraturpris_under_de_50_ar_som_priset_funnits_%283%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0a/Urval_av_de_bocker_som_har_vunnit_Nordiska_radets_litteraturpris_under_de_50_ar_som_priset_funnits_%283%29.jpg/440px-Urval_av_de_bocker_som_har_vunnit_Nordiska_radets_litteraturpris_under_de_50_ar_som_priset_funnits_%283%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Urval_av_de_bocker_som_har_vunnit_Nordiska_radets_litteraturpris_under_de_50_ar_som_priset_funnits_(3).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Book pages</div></div></div>
<p>"Making cases" happens off-line and prior to the book's arrival at the binding line. In the most basic case-making, two pieces of cardboard are placed onto a glued piece of cloth with a space between them into which is glued a thinner board cut to the width of the spine of the book. The overlapping edges of the cloth (about 5/8" all round) are folded over the boards, and pressed down to adhere. After case-making the stack of cases will go to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Foil_stamping" title="Foil stamping">foil stamping</a> area for adding decorations and type.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Digital_printing">Digital printing</span></h2>
<p>Recent developments in book manufacturing include the development of digital printing. Book pages are printed, in much the same way as an office copier works, using <a href="/wiki/Toner" title="Toner">toner</a> rather than ink. Each book is printed in one pass, not as separate signatures. Digital printing has permitted the manufacture of much smaller quantities than offset, in part because of the absence of make readies and of spoilage. One might think of a web press as printing quantities over 2000, quantities from 250 to 2000 being printed on sheet-fed presses, and digital presses doing quantities below 250. These numbers are of course only approximate and will vary from supplier to supplier, and from book to book depending on its characteristics. Digital printing has opened up the possibility of print-on-demand, where no books are printed until after an order is received from a customer.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ebook">Ebook</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Ebook" title="Ebook">Ebook</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Amazon_Kindle_3.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3328" data-file-width="2256" decoding="async" height="251" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/00/Amazon_Kindle_3.JPG/170px-Amazon_Kindle_3.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/00/Amazon_Kindle_3.JPG/255px-Amazon_Kindle_3.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/00/Amazon_Kindle_3.JPG/340px-Amazon_Kindle_3.JPG 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Amazon_Kindle_3.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A screen of a <a href="/wiki/Amazon_Kindle" title="Amazon Kindle">Kindle</a> e-reader.</div></div></div>
<p>In the 2000s, due to the rise in availability of affordable handheld computing devices, the opportunity to share texts through electronic means became an appealing option for media publishers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup> Thus, the "ebook" was made. The term ebook is a contraction of "electronic book"; it refers to a book-length publication in digital form.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup> An ebook is usually made available through the internet, but also on CD-ROM and other forms. Ebooks may be read either via a computing device with an LED display such as a traditional <a href="/wiki/Computer" title="Computer">computer</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Smartphone" title="Smartphone">smartphone</a> or a <a href="/wiki/Tablet_computer" title="Tablet computer">tablet computer</a>; or by means of a portable e-ink display device known as an ebook reader, such as the <a href="/wiki/Sony_Reader" title="Sony Reader">Sony Reader</a>, <a href="/wiki/Barnes_%26_Noble_Nook" title="Barnes &amp; Noble Nook">Barnes &amp; Noble Nook</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kobo_eReader" title="Kobo eReader">Kobo eReader</a>, or the <a href="/wiki/Amazon_Kindle" title="Amazon Kindle">Amazon Kindle</a>. Ebook readers attempt to mimic the experience of reading a print book by using this technology, since the displays on ebook readers are much less reflective.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Design">Design</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Book_design" title="Book design">Book design</a></div>
<p>Book design is the art of incorporating the content, style, format, design, and sequence of the various components of a book into a coherent whole. In the words of Jan Tschichold, book design "though largely forgotten today, methods and rules upon which it is impossible to improve have been developed over centuries. To produce perfect books these rules have to be brought back to life and applied." Richard Hendel describes book design as "an arcane subject" and refers to the need for a context to understand what that means. Many different creators can contribute to book design, including <a href="/wiki/Graphic_design" title="Graphic design">graphic designers</a>, <a href="/wiki/Artist" title="Artist">artists</a> and editors.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Sizes">Sizes</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Book_size" title="Book size">Book size</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Codex_Gigas_facsimile.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1728" data-file-width="2304" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/Codex_Gigas_facsimile.jpg/220px-Codex_Gigas_facsimile.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/Codex_Gigas_facsimile.jpg/330px-Codex_Gigas_facsimile.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/Codex_Gigas_facsimile.jpg/440px-Codex_Gigas_facsimile.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Codex_Gigas_facsimile.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Actual-size <a href="/wiki/Facsimile" title="Facsimile">facsimile</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Codex_Gigas" title="Codex Gigas">Codex Gigas</a>, also known as the 'Devil's Bible' (from the illustration at right)</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Kyauksa.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1600" data-file-width="1200" decoding="async" height="227" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/Kyauksa.JPG/170px-Kyauksa.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/Kyauksa.JPG/255px-Kyauksa.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/Kyauksa.JPG/340px-Kyauksa.JPG 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Kyauksa.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A page from the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/World%27s_largest_book" title="World's largest book">world's largest book</a>. Each page is three and a half feet wide, five feet tall and a little over five inches thick</div></div></div>
<p>The size of a modern book is based on the printing area of a common flatbed press. The pages of type were arranged and clamped in a frame, so that when printed on a sheet of paper the full size of the press, the pages would be right side up and in order when the sheet was folded, and the folded edges trimmed.
</p><p>The most common book sizes are:
</p>
<ul><li>Quarto (4to): the sheet of paper is folded twice, forming four leaves (eight pages) approximately 11–13 inches (c. 30 cm) tall</li>
<li>Octavo (8vo): the most common size for current hardcover books. The sheet is folded three times into eight leaves (16 pages) up to <style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1020198016">.mw-parser-output .frac{white-space:nowrap}.mw-parser-output .frac .num,.mw-parser-output .frac .den{font-size:80%;line-height:0;vertical-align:super}.mw-parser-output .frac .den{vertical-align:sub}.mw-parser-output .sr-only{border:0;clip:rect(0,0,0,0);height:1px;margin:-1px;overflow:hidden;padding:0;position:absolute;width:1px}</style><span class="frac" role="math">9<span class="sr-only">+</span><span class="num">3</span>⁄<span class="den">4</span></span> inches (c. 23 cm) tall.</li>
<li>DuoDecimo (12mo): a size between 8vo and 16mo, up to <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1020198016" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="frac" role="math">7<span class="sr-only">+</span><span class="num">3</span>⁄<span class="den">4</span></span> inches (c. 18 cm) tall</li>
<li>Sextodecimo (16mo): the sheet is folded four times, forming 16 leaves (32 pages) up to <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1020198016" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="frac" role="math">6<span class="sr-only">+</span><span class="num">3</span>⁄<span class="den">4</span></span> inches (c. 15 cm) tall</li></ul>
<p>Sizes smaller than 16mo are:
</p>
<ul><li>24mo: up to <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1020198016" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="frac" role="math">5<span class="sr-only">+</span><span class="num">3</span>⁄<span class="den">4</span></span> inches (c. 13 cm) tall.</li>
<li>32mo: up to 5 inches (c. 12 cm) tall.</li>
<li>48mo: up to 4 inches (c. 10 cm) tall.</li>
<li>64mo: up to 3 inches (c. 8 cm) tall.</li></ul>
<p>Small books can be called <i>booklets</i>.
</p><p>Sizes larger than quarto are:
</p>
<ul><li>Folio: up to 15 inches (c. 38 cm) tall.</li>
<li>Elephant Folio: up to 23 inches (c. 58 cm) tall.</li>
<li>Atlas Folio: up to 25 inches (c. 63 cm) tall.</li>
<li>Double Elephant Folio: up to 50 inches (c. 127 cm) tall.</li></ul>
<p>The largest extant medieval manuscript in the world is <a href="/wiki/Codex_Gigas" title="Codex Gigas">Codex Gigas</a> 92 × 50 × 22 cm. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/World%27s_largest_book" title="World's largest book">world's largest book</a> is made of stone and is in <a href="/wiki/Kuthodaw_Pagoda" title="Kuthodaw Pagoda">Kuthodaw Pagoda</a> (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Burma" title="Burma">Burma</a>).
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Types">Types</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="By_content">By content</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Polish_sci_fi_fantasy_books.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2304" data-file-width="1728" decoding="async" height="227" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5e/Polish_sci_fi_fantasy_books.JPG/170px-Polish_sci_fi_fantasy_books.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5e/Polish_sci_fi_fantasy_books.JPG/255px-Polish_sci_fi_fantasy_books.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5e/Polish_sci_fi_fantasy_books.JPG/340px-Polish_sci_fi_fantasy_books.JPG 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Polish_sci_fi_fantasy_books.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Novel" title="Novel">Novels</a> in a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bookstore" title="Bookstore">bookstore</a></div></div></div>
<p>A common separation by content are <a href="/wiki/Fiction" title="Fiction">fiction</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Non-fiction" title="Non-fiction">non-fiction</a> books. This simple separation can be found in most <a href="/wiki/Collection_(museum)" title="Collection (museum)">collections</a>, <a href="/wiki/Library" title="Library">libraries</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bookstore" title="Bookstore">bookstores</a>. There are other types such as books of <a href="/wiki/Sheet_music" title="Sheet music">sheet music</a>.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Fiction">Fiction</span></h4>
<p>Many of the books published today are "fiction", meaning that they contain invented material, and are creative <a href="/wiki/Literature" title="Literature">literature</a>. Other literary forms such as <a href="/wiki/Poetry" title="Poetry">poetry</a> are included in the broad category. Most fiction is additionally categorized by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Literary_form" title="Literary form">literary form</a> and <a href="/wiki/Genre" title="Genre">genre</a>.
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Novel" title="Novel">novel</a> is the most common form of fiction book. Novels are stories that typically feature a <a href="/wiki/Plot_(narrative)" title="Plot (narrative)">plot</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Setting_(fiction)" title="Setting (fiction)">setting</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Theme_(literature)" title="Theme (literature)">themes</a> and <a href="/wiki/Character_(arts)" title="Character (arts)">characters</a>. Stories and narrative are not restricted to any topic; a novel can be whimsical, serious or <a href="/wiki/Controversy" title="Controversy">controversial</a>. The novel has had a tremendous impact on entertainment and <a href="/wiki/Publishing" title="Publishing">publishing</a> <a href="/wiki/Market_(economics)" title="Market (economics)">markets</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup> A <a href="/wiki/Novella" title="Novella">novella</a> is a term sometimes used for fiction <a href="/wiki/Prose" title="Prose">prose</a> typically between 17,500 and 40,000 words, and a <a href="/wiki/Novella" title="Novella">novelette</a> between 7,500 and 17,500. A <a href="/wiki/Short_story" title="Short story">short story</a> may be any length up to 10,000 words, but these word lengths vary.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Comic_book" title="Comic book">Comic books</a> or <a href="/wiki/Graphic_novel" title="Graphic novel">graphic novels</a> are books in which the story is illustrated. The characters and narrators use speech or thought bubbles to express verbal language.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Non-fiction">Non-fiction</span></h4>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Stefan_Ramult-Pomeranian_Dictionary.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1072" data-file-width="908" decoding="async" height="201" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a4/Stefan_Ramult-Pomeranian_Dictionary.png/170px-Stefan_Ramult-Pomeranian_Dictionary.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a4/Stefan_Ramult-Pomeranian_Dictionary.png/255px-Stefan_Ramult-Pomeranian_Dictionary.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a4/Stefan_Ramult-Pomeranian_Dictionary.png/340px-Stefan_Ramult-Pomeranian_Dictionary.png 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Stefan_Ramult-Pomeranian_Dictionary.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A page from a <a href="/wiki/Dictionary" title="Dictionary">dictionary</a></div></div></div>
<p>Non-fiction books are in principle based on fact, on subjects such as history, politics, social and cultural issues, as well as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Autobiographies" title="Autobiographies">autobiographies</a> and <a href="/wiki/Memoir" title="Memoir">memoirs</a>. Nearly all <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Academic_literature" title="Academic literature">academic literature</a> is non-fiction. A <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Reference_book" title="Reference book">reference book</a> is a general type of non-fiction book which provides information as opposed to telling a story, essay, commentary, or otherwise supporting a point of view.
</p><p>An <a href="/wiki/Almanac" title="Almanac">almanac</a> is a very general reference book, usually one-volume, with lists of data and information on many topics. An <a href="/wiki/Encyclopedia" title="Encyclopedia">encyclopedia</a> is a book or set of books designed to have more in-depth articles on many topics.
