A book listing <a href="/wiki/Word" title="Word">words</a>, their <a href="/wiki/Etymology" title="Etymology">etymology</a>, meanings, and other information is called a <a href="/wiki/Dictionary" title="Dictionary">dictionary</a>. A book which is a collection of <a href="/wiki/Map" title="Map">maps</a> is an <a href="/wiki/Atlas" title="Atlas">atlas</a>. A more specific reference book with tables or lists of data and information about a certain topic, often intended for professional use, is often called a <a href="/wiki/Handbook" title="Handbook">handbook</a>. Books which try to list references and abstracts in a certain broad area may be called an <a href="/wiki/Index_(publishing)" title="Index (publishing)">index</a>, such as <i>Engineering Index</i>, or <a href="/wiki/Abstract_(summary)" title="Abstract (summary)">abstracts</a> such as chemical abstracts and biological abstracts.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Atlas_-_book.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="768" data-file-width="1024" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/34/Atlas_-_book.jpg/220px-Atlas_-_book.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/34/Atlas_-_book.jpg/330px-Atlas_-_book.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/34/Atlas_-_book.jpg/440px-Atlas_-_book.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Atlas_-_book.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>An <a href="/wiki/Atlas" title="Atlas">atlas</a></div></div></div>
<p>Books with technical information on how to do something or how to use some equipment are called <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Instruction_manual" title="Instruction manual">instruction manuals</a>. Other popular <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/How-to" title="How-to">how-to</a> books include <a href="/wiki/Cookbook" title="Cookbook">cookbooks</a> and <a href="/wiki/Home_improvement" title="Home improvement">home improvement</a> books.
</p><p>Students typically store and carry <a href="/wiki/Textbook" title="Textbook">textbooks</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Schoolbooks" title="Schoolbooks">schoolbooks</a> for study purposes.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Unpublished">Unpublished</span></h4>
<p>Many types of book are private, often filled in by the owner, for a variety of personal records. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Elementary_school" title="Elementary school">Elementary school</a> pupils often use <a href="/wiki/Workbook" title="Workbook">workbooks</a>, which are published with spaces or blanks to be filled by them for study or <a href="/wiki/Homework" title="Homework">homework</a>. In US <a href="/wiki/Higher_education" title="Higher education">higher education</a>, it is common for a student to take an exam using a <a href="/wiki/Blue_book_exam" title="Blue book exam">blue book</a>.
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Friedrich_Kellner_diary_Oct_6,_1939_p3.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1807" data-file-width="1689" decoding="async" height="182" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/Friedrich_Kellner_diary_Oct_6%2C_1939_p3.jpg/170px-Friedrich_Kellner_diary_Oct_6%2C_1939_p3.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/Friedrich_Kellner_diary_Oct_6%2C_1939_p3.jpg/255px-Friedrich_Kellner_diary_Oct_6%2C_1939_p3.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/Friedrich_Kellner_diary_Oct_6%2C_1939_p3.jpg/340px-Friedrich_Kellner_diary_Oct_6%2C_1939_p3.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Friedrich_Kellner_diary_Oct_6,_1939_p3.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A page from a <a href="/wiki/Notebook" title="Notebook">notebook</a> used as hand written <a href="/wiki/Diary" title="Diary">diary</a></div></div></div>
<p>There is a large set of books that are made only to write private ideas, notes, and accounts. These books are rarely published and are typically destroyed or remain private. <a href="/wiki/Notebook" title="Notebook">Notebooks</a> are blank papers to be written in by the user. <a href="/wiki/Student" title="Student">Students</a> and <a href="/wiki/Writer" title="Writer">writers</a> commonly use them for taking notes. <a href="/wiki/Scientist" title="Scientist">Scientists</a> and other researchers use <a href="/wiki/Lab_notebook" title="Lab notebook">lab notebooks</a> to record their notes. They often feature spiral coil bindings at the edge so that pages may easily be torn out.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:MichYellowBooks.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="600" data-file-width="800" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/02/MichYellowBooks.JPG/220px-MichYellowBooks.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/02/MichYellowBooks.JPG/330px-MichYellowBooks.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/02/MichYellowBooks.JPG/440px-MichYellowBooks.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:MichYellowBooks.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A <a href="/wiki/Telephone_directory" title="Telephone directory">telephone directory</a>, with business and residence listings.</div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Address_book" title="Address book">Address books</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Phone_book" title="Phone book">phone books</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Meeting" title="Meeting">calendar/appointment</a> books are commonly used on a daily basis for recording appointments, meetings and personal <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Address_(geography)" title="Address (geography)">contact information</a>. Books for recording periodic entries by the user, such as daily information about a journey, are called <a href="/wiki/Logbook" title="Logbook">logbooks</a> or simply logs. A similar book for writing the owner's daily private personal events, information, and ideas is called a <a href="/wiki/Diary" title="Diary">diary</a> or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Personal_journal" title="Personal journal">personal journal</a>. <a href="/wiki/Business" title="Business">Businesses</a> use <a href="/wiki/Accounting" title="Accounting">accounting</a> books such as journals and <a href="/wiki/Ledger" title="Ledger">ledgers</a> to record financial data in a practice called <a href="/wiki/Bookkeeping" title="Bookkeeping">bookkeeping</a> (now usually held on computers rather than in hand-written form).
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Other">Other</span></h4>
<p>There are several other types of books which are not commonly found under this system. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Photograph_album" title="Photograph album">Albums</a> are books for holding a group of items belonging to a particular theme, such as a set of <a href="/wiki/Photograph" title="Photograph">photographs</a>, card collections, and memorabilia. One common example is <a href="/wiki/Stamp_album" title="Stamp album">stamp albums</a>, which are used by many hobbyists to protect and organize their collections of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Postage_stamps" title="Postage stamps">postage stamps</a>. Such albums are often made using removable plastic pages held inside in a ringed binder or other similar holder. Picture books are books for children with pictures on every page and less text (or even no text).
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Hymnal" title="Hymnal">Hymnals</a> are books with collections of <a href="/wiki/Music" title="Music">musical</a> <a href="/wiki/Hymn" title="Hymn">hymns</a> that can typically be found in <a href="/wiki/Church_(building)" title="Church (building)">churches</a>. <a href="/wiki/Breviary" title="Breviary">Prayerbooks</a> or <a href="/wiki/Missal" title="Missal">missals</a> are books that contain written <a href="/wiki/Prayer" title="Prayer">prayers</a> and are commonly carried by <a href="/wiki/Monk" title="Monk">monks</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nun" title="Nun">nuns</a>, and other devoted followers or <a href="/wiki/Clergy" title="Clergy">clergy</a>. <a href="/wiki/Lap_book" title="Lap book">Lap books</a> are a learning tool created by students.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Decodable_readers_and_leveling">Decodable readers and leveling</span></h3>
<p>A leveled book collection is a set of books organized in levels of difficulty from the easy books appropriate for an emergent reader to longer more complex books adequate for advanced readers. Decodable readers or books are a specialized type of leveled books that use decodable text only including controlled lists of words, sentences and stories consistent with the letters and phonics that have been taught to the emergent reader. New sounds and letters are added to higher level decodable books, as the level of instruction progresses, allowing for higher levels of accuracy, comprehension and fluency.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="By_physical_format">By physical format</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Latin_dictionary.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1120" data-file-width="1408" decoding="async" height="175" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6e/Latin_dictionary.jpg/220px-Latin_dictionary.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6e/Latin_dictionary.jpg/330px-Latin_dictionary.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6e/Latin_dictionary.jpg/440px-Latin_dictionary.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Latin_dictionary.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Hardcover" title="Hardcover">Hardcover</a> books</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Borders_bookshelf.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="753" data-file-width="1000" decoding="async" height="166" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Borders_bookshelf.jpg/220px-Borders_bookshelf.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Borders_bookshelf.jpg/330px-Borders_bookshelf.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Borders_bookshelf.jpg/440px-Borders_bookshelf.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Borders_bookshelf.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Paperback" title="Paperback">Paperback</a> books</div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Hardcover" title="Hardcover">Hardcover</a> books have a stiff binding. <a href="/wiki/Paperback" title="Paperback">Paperback</a> books have cheaper, flexible covers which tend to be less durable. An alternative to paperback is the glossy cover, otherwise known as a dust cover, found on magazines, and comic books. Spiral-bound books are bound by spirals made of metal or plastic. Examples of spiral-bound books include teachers' manuals and <a href="/wiki/Puzzle_book" title="Puzzle book">puzzle books</a> (<a href="/wiki/Crossword" title="Crossword">crosswords</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sudoku" title="Sudoku">sudoku</a>).
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Publishing" title="Publishing">Publishing</a> is a process for producing pre-printed books, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Magazines" title="Magazines">magazines</a>, and newspapers for the reader/user to buy.
</p><p>Publishers may produce low-cost, pre-publication copies known as <a href="/wiki/Galley_proof" title="Galley proof">galleys</a> or 'bound proofs' for promotional purposes, such as generating reviews in advance of publication. Galleys are usually made as cheaply as possible, since they are not intended for sale.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Dummy_books">Dummy books</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Cigarette_smuggling_with_a_book.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3072" data-file-width="4608" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0a/Cigarette_smuggling_with_a_book.JPG/220px-Cigarette_smuggling_with_a_book.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0a/Cigarette_smuggling_with_a_book.JPG/330px-Cigarette_smuggling_with_a_book.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0a/Cigarette_smuggling_with_a_book.JPG/440px-Cigarette_smuggling_with_a_book.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Cigarette_smuggling_with_a_book.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Cigarette smuggling with a book</div></div></div>
<p>Dummy books (or faux books) are books that are designed to imitate a real book by appearance to deceive people, some books may be whole with empty pages, others may be hollow or in other cases, there may be a whole panel carved with spines which are then painted to look like books, titles of some books may also be fictitious. 
</p><p>There are many reasons to have dummy books on display such as; to allude visitors of the vast wealth of information in their possession and to inflate the owner's appearance of wealth, to conceal something,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup> for shop displays or for decorative purposes.
</p><p>In early 19th century at <a href="/wiki/Gwrych_Castle" title="Gwrych Castle">Gwrych Castle</a>, <a href="/wiki/North_Wales" title="North Wales">North Wales</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lloyd_Hesketh_Bamford-Hesketh" title="Lloyd Hesketh Bamford-Hesketh">Lloyd Hesketh Bamford-Hesketh</a> was known for his vast collection of books at his library, however, at the later part of that same century, the public became aware that parts of his library was a fabrication, dummy books were built and then locked behind glass doors to stop people from trying to access them, from this a proverb was born, "Like Hesky's library, all outside".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Libraries">Libraries</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Library" title="Library">Library</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Celsus-Bibliothek2.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1944" data-file-width="2592" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a8/Celsus-Bibliothek2.jpg/220px-Celsus-Bibliothek2.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a8/Celsus-Bibliothek2.jpg/330px-Celsus-Bibliothek2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a8/Celsus-Bibliothek2.jpg/440px-Celsus-Bibliothek2.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Celsus-Bibliothek2.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Library_of_Celsus" title="Library of Celsus">Library of Celsus</a> in <a href="/wiki/Ephesus" title="Ephesus">Ephesus</a>, Turkey was built in 135 AD, and could house around 12,000 scrolls.</div></div></div>
<p>Private or personal libraries made up of non-fiction and fiction books, (as opposed to the state or institutional records kept in <a href="/wiki/Archive" title="Archive">archives</a>) first appeared in <a href="/wiki/Classical_Greece" title="Classical Greece">classical Greece</a>. In the ancient world, the maintaining of a <a href="/wiki/Library" title="Library">library</a> was usually (but not exclusively) the privilege of a wealthy individual. These libraries could have been either private or public, i.e. for people who were interested in using them. The difference from a modern <a href="/wiki/Public_library" title="Public library">public library</a> lies in that they were usually not funded from public sources. It is estimated that in the city of Rome at the end of the 3rd century there were around 30 public libraries. Public libraries also existed in other cities of the ancient <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_Mediterranean_region" title="History of the Mediterranean region">Mediterranean region</a> (for example, <a href="/wiki/Library_of_Alexandria" title="Library of Alexandria">Library of Alexandria</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup> Later, in the Middle Ages, monasteries and universities had also libraries that could be accessible to general public. Typically not the whole collection was available to public, the books could not be borrowed and often were chained to reading stands to prevent theft.
</p><p>The beginning of modern public library begins around 15th century when individuals started to donate books to towns.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup> The growth of a <a href="/wiki/Public_library" title="Public library">public library</a> system in the United States started in the late 19th century and was much helped by donations from <a href="/wiki/Andrew_Carnegie" title="Andrew Carnegie">Andrew Carnegie</a>. This reflected classes in a society: The poor or the middle class had to access most books through a public library or by other means while the rich could afford to have a <a href="/wiki/Private_library" title="Private library">private library</a> built in their homes. In the United States the Boston Public Library 1852 <i>Report of the Trustees</i> established the justification for the public library as a tax-supported institution intended to extend educational opportunity and provide for general culture.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup>
</p><p>The advent of <a href="/wiki/Paperback" title="Paperback">paperback</a> books in the 20th century led to an explosion of popular publishing. Paperback books made owning books affordable for many people. Paperback books often included works from genres that had previously been published mostly in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pulp_magazines" title="Pulp magazines">pulp magazines</a>. As a result of the low cost of such books and the spread of bookstores filled with them (in addition to the creation of a smaller market of extremely cheap used paperbacks) owning a private library ceased to be a status symbol for the rich.
</p><p>In library and booksellers' catalogues, it is common to include an abbreviation such as "Crown 8vo" to indicate the <a href="/wiki/Paper_size" title="Paper size">paper size</a> from which the book is made.
</p><p>When rows of books are lined on a book holder, <a href="/wiki/Bookend" title="Bookend">bookends</a> are sometimes needed to keep them from slanting.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Identification_and_classification">Identification and classification</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:ISBN.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="805" data-file-width="1501" decoding="async" height="118" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d2/ISBN.JPG/220px-ISBN.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d2/ISBN.JPG/330px-ISBN.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d2/ISBN.JPG/440px-ISBN.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:ISBN.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/ISBN" title="ISBN">ISBN</a> with <a href="/wiki/Barcode" title="Barcode">barcode</a></div></div></div>
<p>During the 20th century, <a href="/wiki/Librarian" title="Librarian">librarians</a> were concerned about keeping track of the many books being added yearly to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gutenberg_Galaxy" title="Gutenberg Galaxy">Gutenberg Galaxy</a>. Through a global society called the <a href="/wiki/International_Federation_of_Library_Associations_and_Institutions" title="International Federation of Library Associations and Institutions">International Federation of Library Associations and Institutions</a> (IFLA), they devised a series of tools including the <a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Bibliographic_Description" title="International Standard Bibliographic Description">International Standard Bibliographic Description</a> (ISBD). Each book is specified by an International Standard Book Number, or ISBN, which is unique to every edition of every book produced by participating publishers, worldwide. It is managed by the ISBN Society. An ISBN has four parts: the first part is the country code, the second the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Publisher" title="Publisher">publisher</a> code, and the third the title code. The last part is a <a href="/wiki/Check_digit" title="Check digit">check digit</a>, and can take values from 0–9 and X (10). The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/European_Article_Number" title="European Article Number">EAN</a> <a href="/wiki/Barcode" title="Barcode">Barcodes</a> numbers for books are derived from the ISBN by prefixing 978, for <a href="/wiki/Bookland" title="Bookland">Bookland</a>, and calculating a new check digit.
</p><p>Commercial publishers in industrialized countries generally assign ISBNs to their books, so buyers may presume that the ISBN is part of a total international system, with no exceptions. However, many government publishers, in industrial as well as developing countries, do not participate fully in the ISBN system, and publish books which do not have ISBNs. A large or public collection requires a <a href="/wiki/Library_catalog" title="Library catalog">catalogue</a>. Codes called "call numbers" relate the books to the catalogue, and determine their locations on the shelves. Call numbers are based on a <a href="/wiki/Library_classification" title="Library classification">Library classification</a> system. The call number is placed on the spine of the book, normally a short distance before the bottom, and inside. Institutional or national standards, such as <a href="/wiki/American_National_Standards_Institute" title="American National Standards Institute">ANSI</a>/<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/NISO" title="NISO">NISO</a> Z39.41 – 1997, establish the correct way to place information (such as the <a href="/wiki/Title_(publishing)" title="Title (publishing)">title</a>, or the name of the author) on book spines, and on "shelvable" book-like objects, such as containers for <a href="/wiki/DVD" title="DVD">DVDs</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Video_tape" title="Video tape">video tapes</a> and <a href="/wiki/Software" title="Software">software</a>.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Wien_-_Volkskundemuseum-Bibliothek,_B%C3%BCcherspeicher.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2835" data-file-width="4252" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a5/Wien_-_Volkskundemuseum-Bibliothek%2C_B%C3%BCcherspeicher.JPG/220px-Wien_-_Volkskundemuseum-Bibliothek%2C_B%C3%BCcherspeicher.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a5/Wien_-_Volkskundemuseum-Bibliothek%2C_B%C3%BCcherspeicher.JPG/330px-Wien_-_Volkskundemuseum-Bibliothek%2C_B%C3%BCcherspeicher.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a5/Wien_-_Volkskundemuseum-Bibliothek%2C_B%C3%BCcherspeicher.JPG/440px-Wien_-_Volkskundemuseum-Bibliothek%2C_B%C3%BCcherspeicher.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Wien_-_Volkskundemuseum-Bibliothek,_B%C3%BCcherspeicher.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Books on library shelves and call numbers visible on the spines</div></div></div>
<p>One of the earliest and most widely known systems of cataloguing books is the <a href="/wiki/Dewey_Decimal_Classification" title="Dewey Decimal Classification">Dewey Decimal System</a>. Another widely known system is the <a href="/wiki/Library_of_Congress_Classification" title="Library of Congress Classification">Library of Congress Classification</a> system. Both systems are biased towards subjects which were well represented in US libraries when they were developed, and hence have problems handling new subjects, such as computing, or subjects relating to other cultures.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup> Information about books and authors can be stored in databases like <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Online_general-interest_book_database" title="Online general-interest book database">online general-interest book databases</a>. <a href="/wiki/Metadata" title="Metadata">Metadata</a>, which means "data about data" is information about a book. Metadata about a book may include its title, ISBN or other classification number (see above), the names of contributors (author, editor, illustrator) and publisher, its date and size, the language of the text, its subject matter, etc.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Classification_systems">Classification systems</span></h3>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Bliss_bibliographic_classification" title="Bliss bibliographic classification">Bliss bibliographic classification</a> (BC)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Chinese_Library_Classification" title="Chinese Library Classification">Chinese Library Classification</a> (CLC)</li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Colon_Classification" title="Colon Classification">Colon Classification</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Dewey_Decimal_Classification" title="Dewey Decimal Classification">Dewey Decimal Classification</a> (DDC)</li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Harvard-Yenching_Classification" title="Harvard-Yenching Classification">Harvard-Yenching Classification</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Library_of_Congress_Classification" title="Library of Congress Classification">Library of Congress Classification</a> (LCC)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/New_Classification_Scheme_for_Chinese_Libraries" title="New Classification Scheme for Chinese Libraries">New Classification Scheme for Chinese Libraries</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Universal_Decimal_Classification" title="Universal Decimal Classification">Universal Decimal Classification</a> (UDC)</li></ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Uses">Uses</span></h2>
<p>Aside from the primary purpose of reading them, books are also used for other ends:
</p>
<ul><li>A book can be an artistic artifact, a piece of art; this is sometimes known as an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Artists%27_book" title="Artists' book">artists' book</a>.</li>
<li>A book may be evaluated by a reader or professional writer to create a <a href="/wiki/Book_review" title="Book review">book review</a>.</li>
<li>A book may be read by a group of people to use as a spark for social or academic discussion, as in a <a href="/wiki/Book_discussion_club" title="Book discussion club">book club</a>.</li>
<li>A book may be studied by students as the subject of a writing and analysis exercise in the form of a <a href="/wiki/Book_report" title="Book report">book report</a>.</li>
<li>Books are sometimes used for their exterior appearance to <a href="/wiki/Decorative_arts" title="Decorative arts">decorate</a> a room, such as a <a href="/wiki/Study_(room)" title="Study (room)">study</a>.</li></ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Marketing">Marketing</span></h2>
<p>Once the book is published, it is put on the market by the distributors and the bookstores. Meanwhile, his promotion comes from various media reports. Book marketing is governed by the law in many states.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Secondary_spread">Secondary spread</span></h3>
<p>In recent years, the book had a second life in the form of reading aloud. This is called public readings of published works, with the assistance of professional readers (often known actors) and in close collaboration with writers, publishers, booksellers, librarians, leaders of the literary world and artists.
</p><p>Many individual or collective practices exist to increase the number of readers of a book. Among them:
</p>
<ul><li>abandonment of books in public places, coupled or not with the use of the Internet, known as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bookcrossing" title="Bookcrossing">bookcrossing</a>;</li>
<li>provision of free books in third places like bars or cafes;</li>
<li>itinerant or temporary libraries;</li>
<li>free public libraries in the area.</li></ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Industry_evolution">Industry evolution</span></h3>
<p>This form of the book chain has hardly changed since the eighteenth century, and has not always been this way. Thus, the author has asserted gradually with time, and the copyright dates only from the nineteenth century. For many centuries, especially before the invention of printing, each freely copied out books that passed through his hands, adding if necessary his own comments. Similarly, bookseller and publisher jobs have emerged with the invention of printing, which made the book an industrial product, requiring structures of production and marketing.
</p><p>The invention of the Internet, e-readers, tablets, and projects like Wikipedia and Gutenberg, are likely to change the book industry for years to come.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Paper_and_conservation">Paper and conservation</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Conservation_and_restoration_of_books,_manuscripts,_documents_and_ephemera" title="Conservation and restoration of books, manuscripts, documents and ephemera">Conservation and restoration of books, manuscripts, documents and ephemera</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:La_fille_du_Cid_-_Upper_and_lower_cover_and_spine_(c151e11).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2552" data-file-width="3846" decoding="async" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/La_fille_du_Cid_-_Upper_and_lower_cover_and_spine_%28c151e11%29.jpg/220px-La_fille_du_Cid_-_Upper_and_lower_cover_and_spine_%28c151e11%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/La_fille_du_Cid_-_Upper_and_lower_cover_and_spine_%28c151e11%29.jpg/330px-La_fille_du_Cid_-_Upper_and_lower_cover_and_spine_%28c151e11%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/La_fille_du_Cid_-_Upper_and_lower_cover_and_spine_%28c151e11%29.jpg/440px-La_fille_du_Cid_-_Upper_and_lower_cover_and_spine_%28c151e11%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:La_fille_du_Cid_-_Upper_and_lower_cover_and_spine_(c151e11).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Halfbound book with <a href="/wiki/Leather" title="Leather">leather</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Marbled_paper" title="Marbled paper">marbled paper</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>Paper was first made in China as early as 200 BC, and reached Europe through <a href="/wiki/History_of_Islam" title="History of Islam">Muslim</a> territories. At first made of rags, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Industrial_revolution" title="Industrial revolution">industrial revolution</a> changed paper-making practices, allowing for paper to be made out of wood pulp. Papermaking in Europe began in the 11th century, although <a href="/wiki/Vellum" title="Vellum">vellum</a> was also common there as page material up until the beginning of the 16th century, vellum being the more expensive and durable option. Printers or publishers would often issue the same publication on both materials, to cater to more than one market.
</p><p>Paper made from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wood_pulp" title="Wood pulp">wood pulp</a> became popular in the early 20th century, because it was cheaper than <a href="/wiki/Linen" title="Linen">linen</a> or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Abaca" title="Abaca">abaca</a> cloth-based papers. Pulp-based paper made books less expensive to the general public. This paved the way for huge leaps in the rate of literacy in industrialised nations, and enabled the spread of information during the <a href="/wiki/Second_Industrial_Revolution" title="Second Industrial Revolution">Second Industrial Revolution</a>.
</p><p>Pulp paper, however, contains acid which eventually destroys the paper from within. Earlier techniques for making paper used <a href="/wiki/Limestone" title="Limestone">limestone</a> rollers, which neutralized the acid in the pulp. Books printed between 1850 and 1950 are primarily at risk; more recent books are often printed on acid-free or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alkaline" title="Alkaline">alkaline</a> paper. Libraries today have to consider <a href="/wiki/Mass_deacidification" title="Mass deacidification">mass deacidification</a> of their older collections in order to prevent decay.
</p><p>Stability of the climate is critical to the long-term preservation of paper and book material.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup> Good air circulation is important to keep fluctuation in climate stable. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/HVAC" title="HVAC">HVAC</a> system should be up to date and functioning efficiently. Light is detrimental to collections. Therefore, care should be given to the collections by implementing light control. General housekeeping issues can be addressed, including <a href="/wiki/Pest_control" title="Pest control">pest control</a>. In addition to these helpful solutions, a library must also make an effort to be prepared if a disaster occurs, one that they cannot control. Time and effort should be given to create a concise and effective disaster plan to counteract any damage incurred through "acts of God", therefore an <a href="/wiki/Emergency_management" title="Emergency management">emergency management</a> plan should be in place.
</p>
